        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.1c08751"><meta property="og:title" content="Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/acsami.1c08751.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Using first-principles swarm intelligence structure prediction computations, we explore a fully planar BGe monolayer with unique mechanical and electrical properties. Theoretical calculations reveal that a free-standing BGe monolayer has excellent stability, which is confirmed by the cohesive energy (compared to experimentally synthetic borophene and germanene monolayers), phonon modes (no imaginary frequencies appeared in the phonon spectrum), ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations (no broken bonds and geometric reconstructions), and mechanical stability criteria. The metallic feature of the BGe monolayer can be maintained after absorbing different numbers of Na atoms, ensuring good electronic conductivity during the charge/discharge process. The calculated migration energy barrier, open-circuit voltage, and theoretical specific capacity of the BGe monolayer are much better than those of some other two-dimensional (2D) materials. These findings render the BGe monolayer a potential candidate for reversible Na-ion battery anode materials with desirable performance."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/acsami.1c08751.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da87955bbfb325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da87955bbfb325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '983db31c-39d7-44fe-affe-c2f679155105'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da87955bbfb325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da87955bbfb325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08751&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c08751" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="90da87955bbfb325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Planar BGe Monolayer as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c08751", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Ding</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">School of Materials, Zhengzhou University of Aeronautics, Zhengzhou 450015, China</div></div><span Phone: +86-371-61912902.</span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Pei++Ding" data-id="article_author_search">Pei Ding</a></span></div></div></div></span></li></ul><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input class="pub-doi" type="hidden" name="pub-doi" value="10.1021/acsami.1c08751"><a href="/doi/epdf/10.1021/acsami.1c08751?ref=article_openPDF" title="PDF" target="_blank" class="article__btn__secondary" data-id="article_header_OpenPDF"><i class="icon-open-pdf"></i><span>Open PDF</span></a><a href="#_i8" title="Supporting Information" class="article__btn__secondary internalNav" data-id="article_header_SupportingInfo"><i class="icon-supporting-info"></i><span>Supporting Information (1)</span></a><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08751&amp;type=refOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3Fid%3Ddoi%3A10.1021%252Facsami.1c08751%26sid%3Dliteratum%253Aachs%26pmid%3D34152741%26genre%3Darticle%26aulast%3DShao%26date%3D2021%26atitle%3DTwo-Dimensional%2BPlanar%2BBGe%2BMonolayer%2Bas%2Ban%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26jtitle%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26title%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26volume%3D13%26issue%3D25%26spage%3D29764%26epage%3D29769%26issn%3D1944-8244" title="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON" class="issue-item_open-url sfxLink"><img src="/userimages/9157ccea-6c85-4e5f-ac2a-6a9fd9422c17/sfxbutton-180e067c810" alt="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON"></a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-meta"><div class="article_header-meta-left"><input type="hidden" name="journalCodeForjhpLink" value="aamick"><input type="hidden" name="journalNameForjhpLink" value="ACS Applied Materials &amp; href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D29764%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLi%2BShao%252C%2BXiangyang%2BDuan%252C%2BYan%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D25%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c08751%26title%3DTwo-Dimensional%2BPlanar%2BBGe%2BMonolayer%2Bas%2Ban%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D29769%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2021" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/medium/am1c08751_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08751&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Using first-principles swarm intelligence structure prediction computations, we explore a fully planar BGe monolayer with unique mechanical and electrical properties. Theoretical calculations reveal that a free-standing BGe monolayer has excellent stability, which is confirmed by the cohesive energy (compared to experimentally synthetic borophene and germanene monolayers), phonon modes (no imaginary frequencies appeared in the phonon spectrum), ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations (no broken bonds and geometric reconstructions), and mechanical stability criteria. The metallic feature of the BGe monolayer can be maintained after absorbing different numbers of Na atoms, ensuring good electronic conductivity during the charge/discharge process. The calculated migration energy barrier, open-circuit voltage, and theoretical specific capacity of the BGe monolayer are much better than those of some other two-dimensional (2D) materials. These findings render the BGe monolayer a potential candidate for reversible Na-ion battery anode materials with desirable performance.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D29764%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLi%2BShao%252C%2BXiangyang%2BDuan%252C%2BYan%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D25%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c08751%26title%3DTwo-Dimensional%2BPlanar%2BBGe%2BMonolayer%2Bas%2Ban%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D29769%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright Â© 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Anode%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290740&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Monolayers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Monolayers" class="article__tags__link">Monolayers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=First-principles+Calculations&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_first-principles%20calculations">first-principles calculations</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Structural+Prediction&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_structural%20prediction">structural prediction</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Bge+Monolayer&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_BGe%20monolayer">BGe monolayer</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20batteries">Na-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20material">anode material</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Recently, several types of borophenes (two-dimensional (2D) boron monolayers), such as Î²<sub>12</sub>, Ï<sub>3</sub>, and Î± sheets, have been successfully synthesized on Ag(111) surfaces under ultrahigh-vacuum conditions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1â5)</a> As has been revealed by scanning, these borophene lattices can be considered as planar triangular sheets with different patterns of periodic hexagonal holes. Theoretical and experimental investigations have demonstrated that borophenes possess extraordinary mechanical, electronic, optical, and thermal properties <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> and may be used in future nanoelectronics. For example, these monolayer boron structures show anisotropic metallic behavior and anisotropic optical properties, so they can be orientation-controlled for flexible applications. However, it is difficult to exfoliate borophenes from the substrate and an isolated one will be seriously deteriorated, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> which greatly limits their practical applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">Germanene, a hexagonal graphene analogue of germanium, is also a single-atom thick 2D sheet. It has been grown on several metallic substrates <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6â8)</a> or a band gap material, MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> using molecular beam epitaxy. Germanene has a low-buckled conformation with a buckling of about 0.64 Ã, which is different from planar graphene. Its buckled honeycomb structure is caused by the mixed sp<sup>2</sup>âsp<sup>3</sup> hybridized state of GeâGe bonds and a high value of spinâorbit coupling. Various studies show that germanene is zero-gap semimetallic with a Dirac cone and has high electron mobility as graphene. Considering its fascinating properties, germanene has potential applications in nanodevices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">With the rapid growth of clean energy demand, it is a pressing task to improve the performance of present lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) further. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> However, lithium scarcity on earth is a serious problem and restricts its further applications. Sodium (Na) is more abundant and cheaper than Li. So far, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are deemed to be plausible large-scale energy storage systems and competing candidates to replace LIBs. Nevertheless, the bottleneck of NIBs is anode materials because the well-developed ones in LIBs are unsuitable for NIBs due to the larger size of Na than Li. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> Thus, much effort has been devoted to designing and preparing anode materials with good stability, fast kinetics, high capacity, and low cost to develop advanced NIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Two-dimensional (2D) materials have attracted great attention as potential anode materials for their unique morphology. Many 2D materials, including borophenes <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 to 18">(16â18)</a> and germanene, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> are proposed to be suitable for LIBs and NIBs. In this research, we investigate whether B and Ge can form stable 2D compounds with properly enhanced NIB anode properties.</div><div class="NLM_p">Perfect planar configurations have been viewed as a new class of potential candidates for the anode materials of LIBs and NIBs. The ab initio computations show that many planar 2D materials, such as NiC<sub>3</sub> monolayer <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and Net-C18, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> are potentially superior candidates for LIBs and NIBs. For example, Zhu et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> identified a fully planar NiC<sub>3</sub> monolayer as a highly symmetric anode material, and the capacity for Li and Na reaches 1698 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>. Cai et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> predicted a 2D graphene-like carbon allotrope of Net-C18 with a low energy barrier (0.32 eV), low open-circuit voltage (OCV; 0.024 V), and high theoretical specific capacity (403 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>) for LIBs. Although intensive investigations have been conducted, perfect planar structures with excellent performance are still under development in the area of NIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Here, on the basis of theoretical structure prediction and density functional theory (DFT) calculations, we report the exploration of free-standing 2D binary BâGe compositions. As expected, a novel stable 2D BâGe hybrid compound, BGe monolayer, is identified. The stability of the BGe monolayer is confirmed by cohesive energies, phonon spectra, molecular dynamics simulations, and mechanical stability criteria, indicating that it is likely to be experimentally synthesized. Similar to borophenes and germanene, the BGe monolayer shows metallicity with good electronic conductivity. Further calculations indicate that the BGe monolayer exhibits great potential as an anode material for NIBs with a small diffusion energy barrier, low open-circuit voltage, and high capacity.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Computational Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Structural search simulations for BâGe candidate structures were performed for various stoichiometries (B<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ge<i><sub>y</sub></i>, <i>x</i> = 1â4, <i>y</i> = 1â4) using the CALYPSO code. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> In our simulations, both population sizes and number of generations were set to be 30. The best 60% of structures were selected through particle swarm optimization (PSO) to generate the next generation, while the other structures were generated randomly to guarantee structural diversity. We used the structure prototype analysis package <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> to remove the duplicate structures in the predicted structures. The generalized gradient approximation (GGA) in the form of PerdewâBurkeâErnzerhof (PBE) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> was used for the exchangeâcorrelation functional, as implemented in the VASP code. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> The ionâelectron interaction was described by the projector augmented wave (PAW) approach. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The plane wave cutoff energy was set to 500 eV. The MonkhorstâPack <i>k</i>-point grid was set to 7 Ã 7 Ã 1 for the Brillouin zone (BZ) integration during self-consistent calculations of the unit cell and was increased to 11 Ã 11 Ã 1 to obtain an accurate electronic structure. van der Waals (vdW) effects were considered using the DFT-D3 method <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> during all of the calculation processes. A â¼20 Ã vacuum space in the direction perpendicular to the BâGe sheet was adopted to eliminate the appreciable interactions between periodic images. The convergence criteria of total energy and atomic force components were fixed to 1 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup> eV and 0.01 eV Ã<sup>â1</sup>, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The phonon dispersion calculations were performed to study the kinetic stability of the proposed structures on the basis of density functional perturbation theory (DFPT) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> as done in the Phonopy code. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations were employed in the NVT ensemble using time steps of 2.0 fs at different temperatures to evaluate the thermal stability of the BGe monolayer. The temperature was controlled using the NosÃ©âHoover method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> To locate the minimum-energy pathway and energy barriers of Na atom diffusion on the surface of B<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ge<i><sub>y</sub></i> sheets, the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> was employed. Nine images including the initial and final positions were used. Other computational details are given in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The lowest energy configuration of the BGe monolayer obtained from the global minimum structure search and structural optimization is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. Different from germanene with a low-buckled structure, both B and Ge atoms in the BGe sheet are positioned in the same plane, namely, it is a graphene-like hexagonal BâGe lattice. The optimized lattice parameter is 3.475 Ã, which is smaller than those of Î±-borophene <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> (5.2 Ã) and germanene <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> (3.8 Â± 0.2 Ã), as listed in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. The BGe monolayer exhibits a space group of <i>P</i>6Ì<i>m</i>2 and contains 1 B and 1 Ge per unit cell with a BâGe bond length of 2.006 Ã. Each Ge atom is surrounded by three B atoms (vice versa) in the same plane, which means one Ge atom has one redundant valence electron. Here, BGe can be considered as B-doped germanene but with a planar honeycomb superstructure. To gain insights into the stability of the bonding, we plotted the electron localization function (ELF), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> as visualized in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. The abundant electrons are localized with a high ELF value of â¼0.75 in the region between adjacent atoms, suggesting polar covalent BâGe bonds. According to Bader charge analysis, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> each Ge atom donates 0.24 e to B atoms.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/medium/am1c08751_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Top and side views of the optimized 2D BGe sheet with possible adsorption sites and diffusion paths for Na atoms on the surface. (b) Top view of the ELF map of the BGe sheet. (c) Phonon dispersion of the BGe sheet.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08751&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Calculated Lattice Constants (Ã), Cohesive Energy <i>E</i><sub>c</sub> (eV atom<sup>â1</sup>), and Youngâs Modulus <i>Y</i> (N m<sup>â1</sup>) of Several 2D Monolayers</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>Y</i><sub>a</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>Y</i><sub>b</sub></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">BGe</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="2" align="left">3.475</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">6.195</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">182.0</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">207.5</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Ï<sub>3</sub>- borophene</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">4.45</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">6.197</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">187.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">180.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Î±-borophene</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="2" align="left">5.2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">6.235</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">212</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">germanene</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">3.8&nbsp;Â±&nbsp;0.2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.051</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">4.051</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">38.4â¼55.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">graphene</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="2" align="left">2.45</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">9.248</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">353.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The fascinating structure of the BGe monolayer inspired us to further evaluate its stability. The free-standing BGe monolayer has a cohesive energy of 4.654 eV atom<sup>â1</sup>. For comparison, the cohesive energies of the experimentally realized Î²<sub>12</sub>-borophene, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1â4)</a> Ï<sub>3</sub>-borophene, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Î±-borophene, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> and germanene <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> monolayers are 6.195, 6.197, 6.235, and 4.051 eV atom<sup>â1</sup> (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>), respectively, at the same calculated level. That is to say, the <i>E</i><sub>c</sub> of the BGe monolayer is between those of borophenes and germanene. We validated the thermodynamic stability of the free-standing BGe monolayer by calculating its phonon dispersion curves. As plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, all branches have a positive frequency in the whole BZ, demonstrating the dynamical stability of the BGe monolayer. The highest vibration mode of the BGe sheet reaches 950 cm<sup>â1</sup> (28.53 THz), which is lower than those of three borophenes (â¼1200 cm<sup>â1</sup>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 39 to 41">(39â41)</a> but much higher than that of germanene (300 cm<sup>â1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> indicating strong BâGe bonds in this monolayer sheet.</div><div class="NLM_p">Besides, we performed AIMD simulations to estimate the thermal stability of the free-standing BGe sheet. A (7 Ã 7) supercell was adopted for the simulations. After being heated at 300 K for 10 ps, the structure can be kept intact, and the total potential energy fluctuates between â449 and â446 eV during the entire process of structural evolution, suggesting that BGe is thermally stable at room temperature with the planar hexagonal rings unaffected (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Furthermore, our calculations show that the BGe sheet can maintain its framework up to a relatively high temperature of 1000 K, implying good stability under a high-temperature environment. It is worth noting that the distorted structure after the simulation at 1000 K can easily recover the initial planar form after geometry relaxation.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition, we inspected the mechanical properties of the BGe monolayer by calculating its liner elastic constants, which are found to be C<sub>11</sub> = C<sub>22</sub> = 80.0, C<sub>12</sub> = C<sub>21</sub> = 43.2, and C<sub>44</sub>= 18.2 N m<sup>â1</sup>. Based on the mechanical equilibrium conditions: C<sub>11</sub>C<sub>22</sub>âC<sub>12</sub><sup>2</sup> &gt; 0 and C<sub>44</sub> &gt; 0, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> the BGe monolayer is mechanically stable. Moreover, the BGe monolayer shows an isotropic in-plane Youngâs modulus of <i>Y</i> = 56.7 N m<sup>â1</sup>. In comparison, the calculated <i>Y</i> value of graphene is 353.1 N m<sup>â1</sup> at our calculation level (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>), which achieves good agreement with previous computations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a> Although the Youngâs modulus of the BGe monolayer is appreciably smaller than those of three borophorenes (180.8â212 N m<sup>â1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref46" aria-label="reference 39, 40 and 46">(39,40,46)</a> it is comparable to that of germanene (38.4â55.8 N m<sup>â1</sup>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>), proving that the BGe monolayer possesses good mechanical properties. To help distinguish the possible BGe sheet by experiments in the future, the simulated scanning tunneling microscopy (STM) (using the constant height model <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> with a height of 1.0 Ã and a negative bias of 0.05V) image of the BGe sheet is given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> for comparison. Although the solubility of B in Ge <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> (or Ge in B <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a>) is obviously small at all temperatures, we propose a possible synthesis approach to fabricate the BGe monolayer. We simulated the BGe monolayer epitaxially growing on the graphene surface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The lattice mismatch of the 5 Ã 5 BGe monolayer with 7 Ã 7 graphene is less than 1%. Therefore, graphene or metal substrates with weak interaction, small lattice mismatch, and same surface symmetry are beneficial to obtain the BGe monolayer by molecular beam epitaxy (MBE), akin to the growth of borophenes <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1â5)</a> and germanene <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 to 9">(6â9)</a> on substrate surfaces.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electronic band structure and density of states (DOS) of the BGe sheet are illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. The BGe monolayer has one partially occupied band across the Fermi level in the BZ; therefore, the metallic character is observed similar to those reported for borophenes and germanene. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 1 to 10">(1â10)</a> The high DOS peak at the Fermi level confirms electronic conductivity. The more accurate HSE06 hybrid functional <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> only slightly enlarges the band energy dispersion without changing the metallicity of the BGe sheet (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>). The orbital-decomposed band structures show that the bands near the Fermi level are mainly contributed by the 4p<i><sub>z</sub></i> orbital of the Ge atom (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4bâe</a>). So, the metallicity primarily comes from the redundant valence electron of the Ge atom as mentioned above. Moreover, the orbital-decomposed band structures of the BGe monolayer show a degree of orbital overlap between B p<i><sub>x</sub></i> + p<i><sub>y</sub></i>and Ge p<i><sub>x</sub></i> + p<i><sub>y</sub></i> (and between B p<i><sub>z</sub></i> and Ge p<i><sub>z</sub></i>), indicating the covalent interaction between them, which is in agreement with the result of ELF (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/medium/am1c08751_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Band structure and DOS of the BGe monolayer. The Fermi level is set to zero.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08751&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As the discussion above attests, the BGe monolayer exhibits metallic characteristics, which provides an intrinsic advantage for being a promising anode material in NIBs. We first investigated the adsorption behavior of an isolated Na atom on the surface of a 2 Ã 2 Ã 1 BGe supercell, corresponding to a stoichiometry of BGeNa<sub>0.25</sub>. Four typical initial adsorption positions with high symmetry, denoted as H, B, T<sub>B</sub>, and T<sub>Ge</sub>, are considered and shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. H is above the center of hexagon BâGe rings, B is above the midpoint of a BâGe bond, and T<sub>B</sub>/T<sub>Ge</sub> is the on-top site of the B/Ge atom. The H site is identified to be the most stable Na adsorption position with an adsorption energy of â1.83 eV per Na atom and a vertical distance of 1.746 Ã from the Na atom to the BGe sheet, suggesting a strong interaction between the Na atom and the BGe sheet, which is beneficial to inhibit the generation of Na dendrites.</div><div class="NLM_p">To better understand the strong interaction between the Na atom and the BGe monolayer, the charge density difference (CDD) of the H site adsorption structure was calculated and is plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. The CDD is identified as the difference of electronic density between the Na-adsorbed BGe sheet and the isolated BGe sheet as well as Na atoms at their respective positions. The red and blue areas represent electron losses and gains, respectively. It can be seen that there is significant charge transfer from the adatom to the substrate BGe monolayer. The Bader charge analysis shows that the charge transfer is 0.84 e, i.e., the Na atom loses almost all its s electrons and becomes a Na ion.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/medium/am1c08751_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Charge density difference (isovalue: Â±0.15 e Ã<sup>â3</sup>) of BGeNa<sub>0.25</sub>, (b) top and side views of path 1, (c) diffusion energy barrier profiles of the three diffusion paths in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, and (d) DOS of BGeNa<sub>0.25</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08751&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The circuit rate capacity of the electrode material closely correlates the Na-ion mobility.</div><div class="NLM_p">CI-NEB calculations are performed to study the diffusion properties of the Na atom on the surface of the BGe sheet. Three possible paths between two neighboring H sites are investigated, as indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. Path 1 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) has the lowest energy barrier of 0.20 eV with the shortest path length of 3.52 Ã (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), which is higher than that of germanene (0.14 eV) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> but lower than those of Î²<sub>12</sub>-borophene (0.33 eV) and Ï<sub>3</sub>-borophene (0.34 eV) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>). To further compare the diffusion property among several candidate anode materials, the temperature-dependent diffusion constant (<i>D</i>) is calculated. The <i>D</i> of BGe along path 1 is about 150 times faster than that of Î²<sub>12</sub>-borophene at 300 K, implying a high charge/discharge rate of the BGe-based anode material for NIBs. The total DOS of BGeNa<sub>0.25</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d) shows that the BGe sheet still maintains its metallic nature after Na adsorption.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. Diffusion Barrier (eV), Theoretical Capacity (mAh g<sup>â1</sup>), and OCV (V) of Several Promising Anode Materials for NIBs<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t2fn1" aria-label="a"><sup>a</sup></a></div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="left"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">material</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">diffusion barrier</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">capacity</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">OCV</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">BGe</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.20</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1927</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.33</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Î²<sub>12</sub>-borophene</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.33</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1984</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.42</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Ï<sub>3</sub>- borophene</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.34</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1240</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.78</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">germanene</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.14</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">369</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">â</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t2fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t2fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">âââ means data unavailable.</p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">In real conditions, the storage capacity is an important feature for 2D anode materials. Here, we consider different concentrations of Na atoms adsorbed on both sides of the 2 Ã 2 Ã 1 supercell layer by layer, simultaneously. With the increase of Na atoms on the BGe monolayer, Na atoms in the first layer occupy the H sites. When all of the adsorption sites of the first layer are loaded by Na atoms, Na atoms start to occupy the T<sub>Ge</sub> site and form the second adsorption layer, which is also above the triangular center of the first Na layer. The Na adatoms of the third layer are still at H sites as those in the first layer. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows the most stable configurations of BGeNa<sub>2</sub>, BGeNa<sub>4</sub>, and BGeNa<sub>6</sub>. To estimate the interaction between the Na layer and the BGe sheet, we calculate the average adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>ave</sub>) in each layer. The Na adsorption energy of BGeNa<i><sub>m</sub></i> decreases with the increase of <i>m</i> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a), owing to the repulsion forces of neighboring Na atoms. The <i>E</i><sub>ave</sub> of BGeNa<sub>6</sub> is still negative (â0.16 eV), indicating at least three-layer Na atoms can be adsorbed on the BGe monolayer. Accordingly, the theoretical specific capacity <i>C</i> of the BGe sheet can approach 1927 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>. This value is comparable to that of Î²<sub>12</sub>-borophene (1984 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and is superior to some other 2D materials such as Ï<sub>3</sub>-borophene (1240 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and germanene (369 mAh g<sup>â1</sup>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>). As the number of the adsorbed Na atoms goes from 8 (BGeNa<sub>2</sub>) to 24 (BGeNa<sub>6</sub>) on the BGe monolayer, the calculated average open-circuit voltage (OCV) decreases from 0.64 to 0.33 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>). Based on the analyses above, we can conclude that the BGe monolayer might be a promising anode in NIBs.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/medium/am1c08751_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) OCV and <i>E</i><sub>ave</sub> as a function of Na content <i>m</i> in the BGeNa<i><sub>m</sub></i> system, (b) single Na vacancy diffusion energy barrier profile, (c) DOSs of BGeNa<i><sub>m</sub></i> (<i>m</i> = 2, 4, 6), and (d) lattice constant of BGe at different Na concentrations.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08751&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Additionally, we also check the Na diffusion behavior with high Na concentrations. We remove one Na ion from the outer layer of BGeNa<sub>6</sub> and relax the structure again. The calculated energy barrier of the single Na vacancy is 0.16 eV. Compared with that of a single Na ion (0.20 eV), the ion mobility is improved further. Meanwhile, the DOSs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c) and band structures (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) show that all BGeNa<i><sub>m</sub></i> (<i>m</i> = 2, 4, 6) systems are still metallic, securing the requisite electrical conductivity during the entire intercalation/deintercalation process. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, electrons spread out to the outer layer and form a negatively charged cloud, effectively alleviating the repulsive interactions among Na ions.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/medium/am1c08751_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. ELF of the (100) section of (a) BGeNa<sub>2</sub>, (b) BGeNa<sub>4</sub>, and (c) BGeNa<sub>6</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/asset/images/large/am1c08751_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c08751&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">We have further explored the structural distortion of BGe electrodes at different Na concentrations (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). Even at a concentration as high as BGeNa<sub>6</sub>, the calculated lattice parameter is 3.552 Ã, which just rises slightly to 2.2% and is much smaller than the lattice change of the commercialized anode material graphite for LIBs (12%). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> The fluctuation of the lattice constant during the sodiation process is acceptable, indicating another great advantage of the BGe sheet to be anode materials for NIBs in practical application.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">To summarize, a novel BGe monolayer with a graphene-like honeycomb structure has been systematically investigated by means of swarm intelligence structure searching combined with first-principles computations. Its dynamical, thermal, and mechanical stabilities have been confirmed by corresponding stability criteria, revealing high feasibility to be synthesized experimentally. The BGe monolayer can adsorb three layers of Na ions on each side, resulting in a remarkably large specific capacity of 1927 mA hg<sup>â1</sup>. The energy barrier for Na ion diffusion is as low as 0.20 eV, ensuring high diffusivity in the charge/discharge process. The evaluated OCV of the highest concentration BGeNa<sub>6</sub> is 0.33 V. The inherent metallic nature of the BGe monolayer after absorbing different numbers of Na atoms provides good electronic conductivity. These results offer insightful prospects for the potential applications of BGe-based high-performance anode materials in NIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c08751?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c08751</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Some computational details; variation of the total energy in the AIMD simulation and the final MD images for the BGe sheet at 300 and 1000 K; simulated STM image of the BGe sheet; top and side views of the optimized BGe monolayer on the graphene substrate; band structure and PDOS of the BGe monolayer at the HSE06 level and orbital-resolved band structures of the B and Ge atoms; and optimized adsorption configurations and band structures for BGeNa<sub>2</sub>, BGeNa<sub>4</sub>, and BGeNa<sub>6</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c08751/suppl_file/am1c08751_si_001.pdf">am1c08751_si_001.pdf (658.0 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c08751" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
